class="mw-body" id="content" role="main">\n<a id="top"></a>\n<div class="mw-body-content" id="siteNotice"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n<div class="mw-indicator" id="mw-indicator-pp-default"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Protection_policy#semi" title="This article is semi-protected."><img alt="Page semi-protected" data-file-height="512" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Semi-protection-shackle.svg/40px-Semi-protection-shackle.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Astronaut</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Cosmonaut&amp;redirect=no" title="Cosmonaut">Cosmonaut</a>)</span></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Astronaut_(disambiguation)" title="Astronaut (disambiguation)">Astronaut (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Cosmonaut" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Cosmonaut_(disambiguation)" title="Cosmonaut (disambiguation)">Cosmonaut (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg/220px-Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg/330px-Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg/440px-Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> Astronaut <a href="/wiki/Bruce_McCandless_II" title="Bruce McCandless II">Bruce McCandless II</a> using a <a href="/wiki/Manned_Maneuvering_Unit" title="Manned Maneuvering Unit">Manned Maneuvering Unit</a> outside <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger"><i>Challenger</i></a> on shuttle mission <a href="/wiki/STS-41-B" title="STS-41-B">STS-41-B</a> in 1984.</div></div></div>\n<p>An <b>astronaut</b> or <b>cosmonaut</b> is a person trained by a <a href="/wiki/List_of_human_spaceflight_programs" title="List of human spaceflight programs">human spaceflight program</a> to command, pilot, or serve as a crew member of a <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a>. Although generally reserved for professional space travelers, the terms are sometimes applied to anyone who travels into space, including scientists, politicians, journalists, and tourists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-fact-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-utah_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-utah-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until 2002, astronauts were sponsored and trained exclusively by governments, either by the military or by civilian space agencies. With the suborbital flight of the privately funded <a href="/wiki/SpaceShipOne" title="SpaceShipOne">SpaceShipOne</a> in 2004, a new category of astronaut was created: the <a href="/wiki/Commercial_astronaut" title="Commercial astronaut">commercial astronaut</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" tocsection-17"><a href="#Health_risks_of_space_travel"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Health risks of space travel</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Food_and_drink"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Food and drink</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Insignia"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Insignia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Deaths"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Deaths</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#References"><span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alan_Shepard_in_capsule_aboard_Freedom_7_before_launch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4096" data-file-width="2678" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Alan_Shepard_in_capsule_aboard_Freedom_7_before_launch.jpg/170px-Alan_Shepard_in_capsule_aboard_Freedom_7_before_launch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Alan_Shepard_in_capsule_aboard_Freedom_7_before_launch.jpg/255px-Alan_Shepard_in_capsule_aboard_Freedom_7_before_launch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Alan_Shepard_in_capsule_aboard_Freedom_7_before_launch.jpg/340px-Alan_Shepard_in_capsule_aboard_Freedom_7_before_launch.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alan_Shepard_in_capsule_aboard_Freedom_7_before_launch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan Shepard</a> aboard <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_3" title="Mercury-Redstone 3"><i>Freedom&#160;7</i></a></div></div></div>\n<p>The criteria for what constitutes <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">human spaceflight</a> vary. The <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration_A%C3%A9ronautique_Internationale" title="F&#233;d&#233;ration A&#233;ronautique Internationale">F&#233;d&#233;ration A&#233;ronautique Internationale</a> (FAI) Sporting Code for astronautics recognizes only flights that exceed an <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n_line" title="K&#225;rm&#225;n line">altitude of 100 kilometers (62&#160;mi)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> In the United States, professional, military, and commercial astronauts who travel above an altitude of 50 miles (80&#160;km)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> are awarded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronaut_Badge" title="Astronaut Badge">astronaut wings</a>.\n</p><p>As of  17&#160;November&#160;2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Astronaut&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, a total of 552 people from <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_space_travel_by_nationality" title="Timeline of space travel by nationality">36 countries</a> have reached 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi) or more in altitude, of which 549 reached <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> or beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stats1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-stats1-5">[5]</a></sup>\nOf these, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_astronauts" title="List of Apollo astronauts">24 people</a> have traveled beyond low Earth orbit, either to lunar orbit, the lunar surface, or, in one case, a loop around the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Three of the 24&#8211;<a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">Jim Lovell</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Watts_Young" title="John Watts Young">John Young</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugene_Cernan" title="Eugene Cernan">Eugene Cernan</a>&#8211;did so twice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hundred_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-hundred-7">[7]</a></sup> The three current<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (February 2018)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> astronauts who have flown without reaching low Earth orbit are <a href="/wiki/Spaceplane" title="Spaceplane">spaceplane</a> pilots <a href="/wiki/Joseph_A._Walker" title="Joseph A. Walker">Joe Walker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Melvill" title="Mike Melvill">Mike Melvill</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Binnie" title="Brian Binnie">Brian Binnie</a>, who participated in suborbital missions.\n</p><p>As of  17&#160;November&#160;2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Astronaut&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, under the U.S. definition, 558 people qualify as having reached space, above 50 miles (80&#160;km) altitude. Of eight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-15" title="X-15">X-15</a> pilots who exceeded 50 miles (80&#160;km) in altitude, only one exceeded 100 kilometers (about 62 miles).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stats1_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-stats1-5">[5]</a></sup> Space travelers have spent over 41,790 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man_hour" title="Man hour">man-days</a> (114.5 man-years) in space, including over 100 astronaut-days of <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">spacewalks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stats_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-stats-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-void_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-void-9">[9]</a></sup> As of  2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Astronaut&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the man with the longest cumulative time in space is <a href="/wiki/Gennady_Padalka" title="Gennady Padalka">Gennady Padalka</a>, who has spent 879 days in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peggy_Whitson" title="Peggy Whitson">Peggy A. Whitson</a> holds the record for the most time in space by a woman, 377 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paw_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-paw-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Astronaut_ranks_and_positions" title="Astronaut ranks and positions">Astronaut ranks and positions</a></div>\n<p>In 1959, when both the United States and <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> were planning, but had yet to launch humans into space, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> Administrator <a href="/wiki/T._Keith_Glennan" title="T. Keith Glennan">T. Keith Glennan</a> and his Deputy Administrator, Dr. <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Latimer_Dryden" title="Hugh Latimer Dryden">Hugh Dryden</a>, discussed whether spacecraft crew members should be called <i>astronauts</i> or <i>cosmonauts</i>. Dryden preferred "cosmonaut", on the grounds that flights would occur in the <i>cosmos</i> (near space), while the "astro" prefix suggested flight to the stars. Most NASA <a href="/wiki/Space_Task_Group" title="Space Task Group">Space Task Group</a> members preferred "astronaut", which survived by common usage as the preferred American term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> When the Soviet Union launched the first man into space, <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a> in 1961, they chose a term which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglicization" title="Anglicization">anglicizes</a> to "cosmonaut".\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English">English</span></h3>\n<p>In English-speaking nations, a professional space traveler is called an <i>astronaut</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The term derives from the Greek words <i>&#225;stron</i> (&#7940;&#963;&#964;&#961;&#959;&#957;), meaning "star", and <i>nautes</i> (&#957;&#945;&#973;&#964;&#951;&#962;), meaning "sailor". The first known use of the term "astronaut" in the modern sense was by <a href="/wiki/Neil_R._Jones" title="Neil R. Jones">Neil R. Jones</a> in his 1930 short story "The Death\'s Head Meteor". The word itself had been known earlier; for example, in <a href="/wiki/Percy_Greg" title="Percy Greg">Percy Greg</a>\'s 1880 book <i>Across the Zodiac</i>, "astronaut" referred to a spacecraft. In <i>Les Navigateurs de l\'Infini</i> (1925) by <a href="/wiki/J.-H._Rosny_a%C3%AEn%C3%A9" title="J.-H. Rosny a&#238;n&#233;">J.-H. Rosny a&#238;n&#233;</a>, the word <i>astronautique</i> (astronautic) was used. The word may have been inspired by "aeronaut", an older term for an air traveler first applied in 1784 to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balloon_(aircraft)" title="Balloon (aircraft)">balloonists</a>. An early use of "astronaut" in a non-fiction publication is <a href="/wiki/Eric_Frank_Russell" title="Eric Frank Russell">Eric Frank Russell</a>\'s poem "The Astronaut", appearing in the November 1934 <i>Bulletin of the <a href="/wiki/British_Interplanetary_Society" title="British Interplanetary Society">British Interplanetary Society</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first known formal use of the term <a href="/wiki/Astronautics" title="Astronautics">astronautics</a> in the scientific community was the establishment of the annual <a href="/wiki/International_Astronautical_Congress" title="International Astronautical Congress">International Astronautical Congress</a> in 1950, and the subsequent founding of the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronautical_Federation" title="International Astronautical Federation">International Astronautical Federation</a> the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAFpage_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAFpage-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> applies the term astronaut to any crew member aboard NASA spacecraft bound for Earth orbit or beyond. NASA also uses the term as a title for those selected to join its <a href="/wiki/NASA_Astronaut_Corps" title="NASA Astronaut Corps">Astronaut Corps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biopage_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-biopage-16">[16]</a></sup> The European Space Agency similarly uses the term astronaut for members of its <a href="/wiki/European_Astronaut_Corps" title="European Astronaut Corps">Astronaut Corps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESApage_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESApage-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russian">Russian</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Soviet_space_program" title="Soviet space program">Soviet space program</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cosmonauts" title="List of cosmonauts">List of cosmonauts</a></div>\n<p>By convention, an astronaut employed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federal_Space_Agency" title="Russian Federal Space Agency">Russian Federal Space Agency</a> (or its <a href="/wiki/Soviet_space_program" title="Soviet space program">Soviet</a> predecessor) is called a <i>cosmonaut</i> in English texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biopage_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-biopage-16">[16]</a></sup> The word is an anglicisation of the Russian word <i>kosmonavt</i> (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru">&#1082;&#1086;&#1089;&#1084;&#1086;&#1085;&#1072;&#1074;&#1090;</span>, <small>Russian pronunciation:&#160;</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Russian" title="Help:IPA/Russian">[k&#601;sm&#592;&#712;naft]</a></span>), one who works in space outside the Earth\'s atmosphere, a space traveler,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_traveller_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-space_traveller-18">[18]</a></sup> which derives from the Greek words <i>kosmos</i> (&#954;&#972;&#963;&#956;&#959;&#962;), meaning "universe", and <i>nautes</i> (&#957;&#945;&#973;&#964;&#951;&#962;), meaning "sailor". Other countries of the former <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> use variations of the Russian word <i>kosmonavt</i>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Polish</a> <i>kosmonauta</i>.\n</p><p>Coinage of the term <i>kosmonavt</i> has been credited to Soviet aeronautics pioneer <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Tikhonravov" title="Mikhail Tikhonravov">Mikhail Tikhonravov</a> (1900&#8211;1974).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The first cosmonaut was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_Air_Force" title="Soviet Air Force">Soviet Air Force</a> pilot <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a>, also the first person in space. <a href="/wiki/Valentina_Tereshkova" title="Valentina Tereshkova">Valentina Tereshkova</a>, a Russian factory worker, was the first woman in space, as well as the first civilian among the Soviet cosmonaut or NASA astronaut corps to make a spaceflight. On March 14, 1995, <a href="/wiki/Norman_Thagard" title="Norman Thagard">Norman Thagard</a> became the first American to ride to space on board a Russian launch vehicle, and thus became the first "American cosmonaut".\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese">Chinese</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_space_program" title="Chinese space program">Chinese space program</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_astronauts" title="List of Chinese astronauts">List of Chinese astronauts</a></div>\n<p>"Y&#468; h&#225;ng yu&#225;n" (<span lang="zh-CN" title="Chinese language text">&#23431;&#33322;&#21592;</span>, "Space-universe navigating personnel") is used for astronauts and cosmonauts in general,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rus_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-rus-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rus2_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-rus2-22">[22]</a></sup> while "H&#225;ngti&#257;n yu&#225;n" (<span lang="zh-CN" title="Chinese language text">&#33322;&#22825;&#21592;</span>, "navigating <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a> personnel") is used for Chinese astronauts. Here, "H&#225;ngti&#257;n" (<span lang="zh-CN" title="Chinese language text">&#33322;&#22825;</span>) is strictly defined as the navigation of outer space within the local <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">star system</a>, i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">Solar system</a>. The phrase "t&#224;i k&#333;ng r&#233;n" (<span lang="zh-TW" title="Chinese language text">&#22826;&#31354;&#20154;</span>, "spaceman") is often used in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The term <i>taikonaut</i> is used by some English-language news media organizations for professional <a href="/wiki/Chinese_space_program" title="Chinese space program">space travelers from China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The word has featured in the <a href="/wiki/Longman" title="Longman">Longman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English</a> dictionaries,  the latter of which describes it as "a hybrid of the Chinese term <i>taikong</i> (space) and the Greek <i>naut</i> (sailor)"; the term became more common in 2003 when China sent its first astronaut <a href="/wiki/Yang_Liwei" title="Yang Liwei">Yang Liwei</a> into space aboard the <i><a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_5" title="Shenzhou 5">Shenzhou 5</a></i> spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>  This is the term used by <a href="/wiki/Xinhua_News_Agency" title="Xinhua News Agency">Xinhua News Agency</a> in the English version of the Chinese <i><a href="/wiki/People%27s_Daily" title="People\'s Daily">People\'s Daily</a></i> since the advent of the Chinese space program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>  The origin of the term is unclear; as early as May 1998, Chiew Lee Yih (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#36249;&#35041;&#26161;</span>) from <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, used it in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newsgroup" title="Newsgroup">newsgroups</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiew_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-chiew-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiew2_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-chiew2-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_terms">Other terms</span></h3>\n<p>With the rise of <a href="/wiki/Space_tourism" title="Space tourism">space tourism</a>, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federal_Space_Agency" title="Russian Federal Space Agency">Russian Federal Space Agency</a> agreed to use the term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spaceflight_participant" title="Spaceflight participant">spaceflight participant</a>" to distinguish those space travelers from professional astronauts on missions coordinated by those two agencies.\n</p><p>While no nation other than the Russian Federation (and previously the former Soviet Union), the United States, and China have launched a manned spacecraft, several other nations have sent people into space in cooperation with one of these countries. Inspired partly by these missions, other synonyms for astronaut have entered occasional English usage. For example, the term <i>spationaut</i> (French spelling: <i>spationaute</i>) is sometimes used to describe French space travelers, from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>spatium</i> for "space", the <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a> term <i>angkasawan</i> was used to describe participants in the <a href="/wiki/Angkasawan_program" title="Angkasawan program">Angkasawan program</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">Indian Space Research Organisation</a> hope to launch a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISRO_Orbital_Vehicle" title="ISRO Orbital Vehicle">spacecraft</a> in 2022 that would carry <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_human_spaceflight_programme#Terminology" title="Indian human spaceflight programme">vyomanauts</a></i>, coined from the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word for space. In <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, the NASA astronaut <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Kopra" title="Timothy Kopra">Timothy Kopra</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Americans" title="Finnish Americans">Finnish American</a>, has sometimes been referred to as <i>sisunautti</i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a> word <i><a href="/wiki/Sisu" title="Sisu">sisu</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_travel_milestones">Space travel milestones</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gagarin_in_Sweden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="176" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Gagarin_in_Sweden.jpg/150px-Gagarin_in_Sweden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Gagarin_in_Sweden.jpg 1.5x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gagarin_in_Sweden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a>, first human in space (1961)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_612748_Valentina_Tereshkova.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1014" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/RIAN_archive_612748_Valentina_Tereshkova.jpg/150px-RIAN_archive_612748_Valentina_Tereshkova.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/RIAN_archive_612748_Valentina_Tereshkova.jpg/225px-RIAN_archive_612748_Valentina_Tereshkova.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/RIAN_archive_612748_Valentina_Tereshkova.jpg/300px-RIAN_archive_612748_Valentina_Tereshkova.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_612748_Valentina_Tereshkova.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Valentina_Tereshkova" title="Valentina Tereshkova">Valentina Tereshkova</a>, first woman in space (1963)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neil_Armstrong_pose.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4026" data-file-width="3300" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Neil_Armstrong_pose.jpg/150px-Neil_Armstrong_pose.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Neil_Armstrong_pose.jpg/225px-Neil_Armstrong_pose.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Neil_Armstrong_pose.jpg/300px-Neil_Armstrong_pose.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neil_Armstrong_pose.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a>, first human to walk on the Moon (1969)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sally_Ride_in_1984.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2850" data-file-width="2244" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Sally_Ride_in_1984.jpg/150px-Sally_Ride_in_1984.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Sally_Ride_in_1984.jpg/225px-Sally_Ride_in_1984.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Sally_Ride_in_1984.jpg/300px-Sally_Ride_in_1984.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sally_Ride_in_1984.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dr. <a href="/wiki/Sally_Ride" title="Sally Ride">Sally Ride</a>, the first American female astronaut (1980s)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yang_Liwei.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4896" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Yang_Liwei.jpg/150px-Yang_Liwei.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Yang_Liwei.jpg/225px-Yang_Liwei.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Yang_Liwei.jpg/300px-Yang_Liwei.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yang_Liwei.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yang_Liwei" title="Yang Liwei">Yang Liwei</a>, first person sent into space by <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (2003)</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spaceflight_records" title="Spaceflight records">Spaceflight records</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_space_travel_by_nationality" title="Timeline of space travel by nationality">Timeline of space travel by nationality</a></div>\n<p>The first human in space was Soviet <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a>, who was launched on April 12, 1961, aboard <a href="/wiki/Vostok_1" title="Vostok 1">Vostok 1</a> and orbited around the Earth for 108 minutes. The first woman in space was Soviet <a href="/wiki/Valentina_Tereshkova" title="Valentina Tereshkova">Valentina Tereshkova</a>, who launched on June 16, 1963, aboard <a href="/wiki/Vostok_6" title="Vostok 6">Vostok 6</a> and orbited Earth for almost three days.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan Shepard</a> became the first American and second person in space on May 5, 1961, on a 15-minute sub-orbital flight. The first American to orbit the Earth was <a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">John Glenn</a>, aboard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friendship_7" title="Friendship 7">Friendship 7</a> on February 20, 1962. The first American woman in space was <a href="/wiki/Sally_Ride" title="Sally Ride">Sally Ride</a>, during <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger">Space Shuttle Challenger</a>\'s mission <a href="/wiki/STS-7" title="STS-7">STS-7</a>, on June 18, 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ride1_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-ride1-30">[30]</a></sup> In 1992 <a href="/wiki/Mae_Jemison" title="Mae Jemison">Mae Jemison</a> became the first African American woman to travel in space aboard <a href="/wiki/STS-47" title="STS-47">STS-47</a>.\n</p><p>Cosmonaut <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexei_Leonov" title="Alexei Leonov">Alexei Leonov</a> was the first person to conduct an <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">extravehicular activity</a> (EVA), (commonly called a "spacewalk"), on March 18, 1965, on the Soviet Union\'s Voskhod 2 mission.  This was followed two and a half months later by astronaut <a href="/wiki/Ed_White_(astronaut)" title="Ed White (astronaut)">Ed White</a> who made the first American EVA on NASA\'s Gemini 4 mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first manned mission to orbit the Moon, <i><a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a></i>, included American <a href="/wiki/William_Anders" title="William Anders">William Anders</a> who was born in Hong Kong, making him the first Asian-born astronaut in 1968.\n</p><p>The Soviet Union, through its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intercosmos" title="Intercosmos">Intercosmos</a> program, allowed people from other "<a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a>" (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> and other Soviet-allied) countries to fly on its missions, with the notable exception of <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> participating in <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_TM-7" title="Soyuz TM-7">Soyuz TM-7</a>. An example is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Czechoslovak" title="Czechoslovak">Czechoslovak</a> <a href="/wiki/Vladim%C3%ADr_Remek" title="Vladim&#237;r Remek">Vladim&#237;r Remek</a>, the first cosmonaut from a country other than the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_space_program" title="Soviet space program">Soviet Union</a> or the <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">United States</a>, who flew to space in 1978 on a <a href="/wiki/Soyuz-U" title="Soyuz-U">Soyuz-U</a> rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-enc-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rakesh_Sharma" title="Rakesh Sharma">Rakesh Sharma</a> became the first Indian citizen to travel to space. He was launched aboard Soyuz T-11, on 2 April 1984.\n</p><p>On July 23, 1980, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pham_Tuan" title="Pham Tuan">Pham Tuan</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> became the first <a href="/wiki/Asian_people" title="Asian people">Asian</a> in space when he flew aboard <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_37" title="Soyuz 37">Soyuz 37</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tuan_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-tuan-33">[33]</a></sup> Also in 1980, <a href="/wiki/Cubans" title="Cubans">Cuban</a> <a href="/wiki/Arnaldo_Tamayo_M%C3%A9ndez" title="Arnaldo Tamayo M&#233;ndez">Arnaldo Tamayo M&#233;ndez</a> became the first person of <a href="/wiki/Hispanic" title="Hispanic">Hispanic</a> and black African descent to fly in space, and in 1983, <a href="/wiki/Guion_Bluford" title="Guion Bluford">Guion Bluford</a> became the first African American to fly into space. In April 1985, <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Wang" title="Taylor Wang">Taylor Wang</a> became the first ethnic Chinese person in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylorbio_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylorbio-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor-35">[35]</a></sup> The first person born in Africa to fly in space was <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Baudry" title="Patrick Baudry">Patrick Baudry</a> (France), in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mendez_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-mendez-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baudry_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-baudry-37">[37]</a></sup> In 1985, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultan_Salman_al-Saud" title="Sultan Salman al-Saud">Prince Sultan Bin Salman Bin AbdulAziz Al-Saud</a> became the first Arab Muslim astronaut in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MC_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-MC-38">[38]</a></sup> In 1988, <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Ahad_Mohmand" title="Abdul Ahad Mohmand">Abdul Ahad Mohmand</a> became the first <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghan</a> to reach space, spending nine days aboard the <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a> space station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the increase of seats on the Space Shuttle, the U.S. began taking international astronauts. In 1983, <a href="/wiki/Ulf_Merbold" title="Ulf Merbold">Ulf Merbold</a> of West Germany became the first non-US citizen to fly in a US spacecraft.  In 1984, <a href="/wiki/Marc_Garneau" title="Marc Garneau">Marc Garneau</a> became the first of 8 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_astronauts" title="Canadian astronauts">Canadian astronauts</a> to fly in space (through 2010).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>\nIn 1985, <a href="/wiki/Rodolfo_Neri_Vela" title="Rodolfo Neri Vela">Rodolfo Neri Vela</a> became the first Mexican-born person in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vela_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-vela-41">[41]</a></sup> In 1991, <a href="/wiki/Helen_Sharman" title="Helen Sharman">Helen Sharman</a> became the first Briton to fly in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcsharmon_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcsharmon-42">[42]</a></sup>\nIn 2002, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Shuttleworth" title="Mark Shuttleworth">Mark Shuttleworth</a> became the first citizen of an African country to fly in space, as a paying spaceflight participant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mark_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-mark-43">[43]</a></sup> In 2003, <a href="/wiki/Ilan_Ramon" title="Ilan Ramon">Ilan Ramon</a> became the first Israeli to fly in space, although he died during a <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Columbia disaster">re-entry accident</a>.\n</p><p>On October 15, 2003, <a href="/wiki/Yang_Liwei" title="Yang Liwei">Yang Liwei</a> became China\'s first astronaut on the <a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_5" title="Shenzhou 5">Shenzhou 5</a> spacecraft.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_milestones">Age milestones</span></h3>\n<p>The youngest person to fly in space is <a href="/wiki/Gherman_Titov" title="Gherman Titov">Gherman Titov</a>, who was 25 years old when he flew <a href="/wiki/Vostok_2" title="Vostok 2">Vostok 2</a>. (Titov was also the first person to suffer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_sickness" title="Space sickness">space sickness</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-age_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-age-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-titov_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-titov-45">[45]</a></sup>\nThe oldest person who has flown in space is <a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">John Glenn</a>, who was 77 when he flew on <a href="/wiki/STS-95" title="STS-95">STS-95</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glenn_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-glenn-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Duration_and_distance_milestones">Duration and distance milestones</span></h3>\n<p>438 days is the longest time spent in space, by Russian <a href="/wiki/Valeri_Polyakov" title="Valeri Polyakov">Valeri Polyakov</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stats_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-stats-8">[8]</a></sup>\nAs of 2006, the most spaceflights by an individual astronaut is seven, a record held by both <a href="/wiki/Jerry_L._Ross" title="Jerry L. Ross">Jerry L. Ross</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franklin_Chang-Diaz" title="Franklin Chang-Diaz">Franklin Chang-Diaz</a>. The farthest distance from Earth an astronaut has traveled was 401,056&#160;km (249,205&#160;mi), when <a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">Jim Lovell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Swigert" title="Jack Swigert">Jack Swigert</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fred_Haise" title="Fred Haise">Fred Haise</a> went around the Moon during the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">Apollo 13</a> emergency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stats_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-stats-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civilian_and_non-government_milestones">Civilian and non-government milestones</span></h3>\n<p>The first civilian in space was <a href="/wiki/Valentina_Tereshkova" title="Valentina Tereshkova">Valentina Tereshkova</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Valentina_Vladimirovna_TERESHKOVA_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Valentina_Vladimirovna_TERESHKOVA-47">[47]</a></sup> aboard <a href="/wiki/Vostok_6" title="Vostok 6">Vostok 6</a> (she also became the first woman in space on that mission).\nTereshkova was only honorarily inducted into the USSR\'s Air Force, which did not accept female pilots at that time. A month later, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Albert_Walker" title="Joseph Albert Walker">Joseph Albert Walker</a> became the first American civilian in space when his <a href="/wiki/X-15_Flight_90" title="X-15 Flight 90">X-15 Flight 90</a> crossed the 100 kilometers (54 nautical miles) line, qualifying him by the international definition of spaceflight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Civilians_in_Space_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Civilians_in_Space-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space.com_Joseph_A_Walker_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space.com_Joseph_A_Walker-49">[49]</a></sup>  Walker had joined the US Army Air Force but was not a member during his flight. \nThe first people in space who had never been a member of any country\'s armed forces were both <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Feoktistov" title="Konstantin Feoktistov">Konstantin Feoktistov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boris_Yegorov" title="Boris Yegorov">Boris Yegorov</a> aboard <a href="/wiki/Voskhod_1" title="Voskhod 1">Voskhod 1</a>.\n</p><p>The first non-governmental space traveler was <a href="/wiki/Byron_K._Lichtenberg" title="Byron K. Lichtenberg">Byron K. Lichtenberg</a>,  a researcher from the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> who flew on <a href="/wiki/STS-9" title="STS-9">STS-9</a> in 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lichten_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-lichten-50">[50]</a></sup> In December 1990, <a href="/wiki/Toyohiro_Akiyama" title="Toyohiro Akiyama">Toyohiro Akiyama</a> became the first paying space traveler as a reporter for <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Broadcasting_System" title="Tokyo Broadcasting System">Tokyo Broadcasting System</a>, a visit to <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a> as part of an estimated $12 million (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USD" title="USD">USD</a>) deal with a Japanese TV station, although at the time, the term used to refer to Akiyama was "Research Cosmonaut".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc1_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc1-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-de-53">[53]</a></sup> Akiyama suffered severe <a href="/wiki/Space_adaptation_syndrome" title="Space adaptation syndrome">space sickness</a> during his mission, which affected his productivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc1_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc1-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first self-funded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_tourist" title="Space tourist">space tourist</a> was <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Tito" title="Dennis Tito">Dennis Tito</a> on board the Russian spacecraft Soyuz TM-3 on April 28, 2001.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-funded_travelers">Self-funded travelers</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_tourism" title="Space tourism">Space tourism</a></div>\n<p>The first person to fly on an entirely privately funded mission was <a href="/wiki/Mike_Melvill" title="Mike Melvill">Mike Melvill</a>, piloting <a href="/wiki/SpaceShipOne_flight_15P" title="SpaceShipOne flight 15P">SpaceShipOne flight 15P</a> on a suborbital journey, although he was a <a href="/wiki/Test_pilot" title="Test pilot">test pilot</a> employed by <a href="/wiki/Scaled_Composites" title="Scaled Composites">Scaled Composites</a> and not an actual paying space tourist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eve_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-eve-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rci_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-rci-55">[55]</a></sup> Seven others have paid the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Space_Agency" title="Russian Space Agency">Russian Space Agency</a> to fly into space:\n</p>\n<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Dennis_Tito" title="Dennis Tito">Dennis Tito</a> (American): April 28 &#8211; May 6, 2001 (<a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">ISS</a>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mark_Shuttleworth" title="Mark Shuttleworth">Mark Shuttleworth</a> (South African): April 25 &#8211; May 5, 2002 (ISS)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gregory_Olsen" title="Gregory Olsen">Gregory Olsen</a> (American): October 1&#8211;11, 2005 (ISS)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Anousheh_Ansari" title="Anousheh Ansari">Anousheh Ansari</a> (Iranian / American): September 18&#8211;29, 2006 (ISS)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Charles_Simonyi" title="Charles Simonyi">Charles Simonyi</a> (Hungarian / American): April 7&#8211;21, 2007 (ISS), March 26 &#8211; April 8, 2009 (ISS)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Richard_Garriott" title="Richard Garriott">Richard Garriott</a> (British / American): October 12&#8211;24, 2008 (ISS)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Guy_Lalibert%C3%A9" title="Guy Lalibert&#233;">Guy Lalibert&#233;</a> (Canadian): September 30, 2009 &#8211; October 11, 2009 (ISS)</li></ol>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Training">Training</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_5_Elliot_See_water_egress_training.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5790" data-file-width="3968" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gemini_5_Elliot_See_water_egress_training.jpg/170px-Gemini_5_Elliot_See_water_egress_training.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gemini_5_Elliot_See_water_egress_training.jpg/255px-Gemini_5_Elliot_See_water_egress_training.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gemini_5_Elliot_See_water_egress_training.jpg/340px-Gemini_5_Elliot_See_water_egress_training.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_5_Elliot_See_water_egress_training.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Elliot_See" title="Elliot See">Elliot See</a> during water egress training with NASA (1965)</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronaut_Training" title="Astronaut Training">Astronaut Training</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Astronaut_ranks_and_positions" title="Astronaut ranks and positions">Astronaut ranks and positions</a></div>\n<p>The first NASA astronauts were selected for training in 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-comet_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-comet-56">[56]</a></sup>  Early in the space program, military jet test piloting and engineering training were often cited as prerequisites for selection as an astronaut at NASA, although neither John Glenn nor Scott Carpenter (of the <a href="/wiki/Mercury_Seven" title="Mercury Seven">Mercury Seven</a>) had any university degree, in engineering or any other discipline at the time of their selection.  Selection was initially limited to military pilots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-training_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-training-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nolen_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-nolen-58">[58]</a></sup> The earliest astronauts for both America and the USSR tended to be <a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">jet fighter</a> pilots, and were often test pilots.\n</p><p>Once selected, NASA astronauts go through twenty months of training in a variety of areas, including training for <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">extravehicular activity</a> in a facility such as NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Neutral_Buoyancy_Laboratory" title="Neutral Buoyancy Laboratory">Neutral Buoyancy Laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-fact-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-training_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-training-57">[57]</a></sup> Astronauts-in-training (astronaut candidates) may also experience short periods of <a href="/wiki/Weightlessness" title="Weightlessness">weightlessness</a> (<a href="/wiki/Micro-g_environment" title="Micro-g environment">microgravity</a>) in an aircraft called the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vomit_Comet" title="Vomit Comet">Vomit Comet</a>," the nickname given to a pair of modified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KC-135" title="KC-135">KC-135s</a> (retired in 2000 and 2004, respectively, and replaced in 2005 with a <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_C-9" title="McDonnell Douglas C-9">C-9</a>) which perform <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabolic</a> flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-comet_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-comet-56">[56]</a></sup> Astronauts are also required to accumulate a number of flight hours in high-performance jet aircraft. This is mostly done in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-38_Talon" title="T-38 Talon">T-38 jet aircraft</a> out of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ellington_Field" title="Ellington Field">Ellington Field</a>, due to its proximity to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">Johnson Space Center</a>.  Ellington Field is also where the <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_Training_Aircraft" title="Shuttle Training Aircraft">Shuttle Training Aircraft</a> is maintained and developed, although most flights of the aircraft are conducted from <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Air_Force_Base" title="Edwards Air Force Base">Edwards Air Force Base</a>.\n</p><p>Astronauts is training must learn how to control and fly the Space Shuttle and, it is vital that they are familiar with the International Space Station so they know what they must do when they get there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NASA_candidacy_requirements">NASA candidacy requirements</span></h3>\n<ul><li>Be citizens of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-comet_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-comet-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-program_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-program-60">[60]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Pass a strict physical examination, and have a near and distant visual acuity correctable to 20/20 (6/6). Blood pressure, while sitting, must be no greater than 140 over 90. There are currently no age restrictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Commander_and_Pilot">Commander and Pilot</span></h4>\n<ul><li>A <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor\'s degree">bachelor\'s degree</a> in <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biological science</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_science" title="Physical science">physical science</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> is required.</li>\n<li>At least 1,000 hours\' flying time as pilot-in-command in jet aircraft. Experience as a test pilot is desirable.</li>\n<li>Height must be 5&#160;ft 2 in to 6&#160;ft 2 in (1.58&#160;m to 1.88&#160;m).</li>\n<li>Distant visual acuity must be correctable to 20/20 in each eye.</li>\n<li>The refractive surgical procedures of the eye, PRK (<a href="/wiki/Photorefractive_keratectomy" title="Photorefractive keratectomy">Photorefractive keratectomy</a>) and <a href="/wiki/LASIK" title="LASIK">LASIK</a>, are now allowed, providing at least 1 year has passed since the date of the procedure with no permanent adverse after effects.  For those applicants under final consideration, an operative report on the surgical procedure will be requested.</li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_Specialist">Mission Specialist</span></h4>\n<ul><li>A bachelor\'s degree in <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_science" title="Biological science">biological science</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_science" title="Physical science">physical science</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, as well as at least three years of related professional experience (graduate work or studies) and an advanced degree, such as a master\'s degree (one to three years) or a doctoral degree (three years or more).</li>\n<li>Applicant\'s height must be between 4&#160;ft 10.5 in and 6&#160;ft 4 in (1.49&#160;m and 1.93&#160;m).</li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_Specialist_Educator">Mission Specialist Educator</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Educator_Astronaut_Project" title="Educator Astronaut Project">Educator Astronaut Project</a></div>\n<ul><li>Applicants must have a bachelor\'s degree with teaching experience, including work at the kindergarten through twelfth grade level. An advanced degree, such as a master\'s degree or a doctoral degree, is not required, but is strongly desired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-announce_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-announce-62">[62]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Educator_Astronaut_Project" title="Educator Astronaut Project">Mission Specialist Educators</a>, or "Educator Astronauts", were first selected in 2004, and as of 2007, there are three NASA Educator astronauts: <a href="/wiki/Joseph_M._Acaba" title="Joseph M. Acaba">Joseph M. Acaba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_R._Arnold" title="Richard R. Arnold">Richard R. Arnold</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Metcalf-Lindenburger" title="Dorothy Metcalf-Lindenburger">Dorothy Metcalf-Lindenburger</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nexgen_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-nexgen-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ed_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-ed-64">[64]</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/Barbara_Morgan" title="Barbara Morgan">Barbara Morgan</a>, selected as back-up teacher to <a href="/wiki/Christa_McAuliffe" title="Christa McAuliffe">Christa McAuliffe</a> in 1985, is considered to be the first Educator astronaut by the media, but she trained as a mission specialist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morgan1_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-morgan1-65">[65]</a></sup>\nThe Educator Astronaut program is a successor to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teacher_in_Space" title="Teacher in Space">Teacher in Space</a> program from the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fly_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-fly-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_risks_of_space_travel">Health risks of space travel</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Padalka_Fincke_ISS_ultrasound.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="990" data-file-width="1190" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Padalka_Fincke_ISS_ultrasound.jpg/220px-Padalka_Fincke_ISS_ultrasound.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Padalka_Fincke_ISS_ultrasound.jpg/330px-Padalka_Fincke_ISS_ultrasound.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Padalka_Fincke_ISS_ultrasound.jpg/440px-Padalka_Fincke_ISS_ultrasound.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Padalka_Fincke_ISS_ultrasound.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gennady_Padalka" title="Gennady Padalka">Gennady Padalka</a> performing ultrasound on <a href="/wiki/Michael_Fincke" title="Michael Fincke">Michael Fincke</a> during ISS <a href="/wiki/Expedition_9" title="Expedition 9">Expedition 9</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body" title="Effect of spaceflight on the human body">Effect of spaceflight on the human body</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space_medicine" title="Space medicine">Space medicine</a></div>\n<p>Astronauts are susceptible to a variety of health risks including <a href="/wiki/Decompression_sickness" title="Decompression sickness">decompression sickness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barotrauma" title="Barotrauma">barotrauma</a>, immunodeficiencies, loss of bone and muscle, loss of eyesight, <a href="/wiki/Orthostatic_intolerance" title="Orthostatic intolerance">orthostatic intolerance</a>, sleep disturbances, and radiation injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20140127_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140127-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired-20120723_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wired-20120723-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mader-2011_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mader-2011-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Puiu-20111109_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Puiu-20111109-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN-20120109_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-20120109-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20120313_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20120313-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radiology-20120313_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Radiology-20120313-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WIRED-20140212_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-WIRED-20140212-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SKR-20171103_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-SKR-20171103-77">[77]</a></sup> A variety of large scale medical studies are being conducted in space via the National Space and Biomedical Research Institute (NSBRI) to address these issues. Prominent among these is the <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Diagnostic_Ultrasound_in_Microgravity" title="Advanced Diagnostic Ultrasound in Microgravity">Advanced Diagnostic Ultrasound in Microgravity</a> Study in which astronauts (including former ISS commanders <a href="/wiki/Leroy_Chiao" title="Leroy Chiao">Leroy Chiao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gennady_Padalka" title="Gennady Padalka">Gennady Padalka</a>) perform ultrasound scans under the guidance of remote experts to diagnose and potentially treat hundreds of medical conditions in space. This study\'s techniques are now being applied to cover professional and Olympic sports injuries as well as ultrasound performed by non-expert operators in medical and high school students. It is anticipated that remote guided ultrasound will have application on Earth in emergency and rural care situations, where access to a trained physician is often rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2006 Space Shuttle experiment found that <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salmonella_typhimurium" title="Salmonella typhimurium">Salmonella typhimurium</a></i>, a bacterium that can cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_poisoning" title="Food poisoning">food poisoning</a>, became more virulent when cultivated in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASU-20070923_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASU-20070923-81">[81]</a></sup> More recently, in 2017, <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> were found to be more resistant to <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> and to thrive in the near-weightlessness of space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GZM-20170913_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-GZM-20170913-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">Microorganisms</a> have been observed to survive the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> of outer space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dose_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dose-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horneck_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horneck-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On December 31, 2012, a <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>-supported study reported that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manned_spaceflight" title="Manned spaceflight">manned spaceflight</a> may harm the brain and accelerate the onset of <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer\'s disease">Alzheimer\'s disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLOS-20121231_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-PLOS-20121231-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpaceRef-20130101_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpaceRef-20130101-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NasaWatch-20130103_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-NasaWatch-20130103-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2015, the <a href="/wiki/NASA_Office_of_Inspector_General" title="NASA Office of Inspector General">NASA Office of Inspector General</a> issued a <a href="/wiki/Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body" title="Effect of spaceflight on the human body">health hazards report</a> related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_space_exploration" title="Human space exploration">space exploration</a>, including a <a href="/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars" title="Human mission to Mars">human mission to Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151029_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151029-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20151029oig_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151029oig-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Over the last decade, flight surgeons and scientists at NASA have seen a pattern of vision problems in astronauts on long-duration space missions. The syndrome, known as visual impairment intracranial pressure (VIIP), has been reported in nearly two-thirds of space explorers after long periods spent aboard the International Space Station (ISS).\n</p><p>On November 2, 2017, scientists reported that significant changes in the position and structure of the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> have been found in astronauts who have taken <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">trips in space</a>, based on <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">MRI studies</a>. Astronauts who took longer space trips were associated with greater brain changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM-20171102_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM-20171102-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-QTZ-20171103_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-QTZ-20171103-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Being in space can be physiologically deconditioning on the body. It can affect the otolith organs and adaptive capabilities of the central nervous system. Zero gravity and cosmic rays can cause many implications for astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2018, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>-funded researchers found that lengthy journeys into <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>, including travel to the <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">planet Mars</a>, may substantially damage the  <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tissues</a> of astronauts. The studies support earlier work that found such journeys could significantly damage the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brains</a> of astronauts, and age them prematurely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TI-20181002_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-TI-20181002-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Researchers in 2018 reported, after detecting the presence on the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS) of five <i><a href="/wiki/Enterobacter" title="Enterobacter">Enterobacter bugandensis</a></i> bacterial strains, none <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogenic</a> to humans, that <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> on ISS should be carefully monitored to continue assuring a medically healthy environment for astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EA-20181122_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-EA-20181122-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMC-20181123_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMC-20181123-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_and_drink">Food and drink</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_food" title="Space food">Space food</a></div>\n<p>An astronaut on the International Space Station requires about 0.83 kilograms (1.83 pounds) weight of food inclusive of food packaging per meal each day. (The packaging for each meal weighs around 0.12 kilograms - 0.27 pounds)  Longer-duration missions require more food.\n</p><p>Shuttle astronauts worked with nutritionists to select menus that appeal to their individual tastes. Five months before flight, menus are selected and analyzed for nutritional content by the shuttle dietician. Foods are tested to see how they will react in a reduced gravity environment.  Caloric requirements are determined using a basal energy expenditure (BEE) formula.\nOn Earth, the average American uses about 35 gallons (132 liters) of water every day.  On board the ISS astronauts limit water use to only about three gallons (11 liters) per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Insignia">Insignia</span></h2>\n<p>In Russia, cosmonauts are awarded <a href="/wiki/Pilot-Cosmonaut_of_the_Russian_Federation" title="Pilot-Cosmonaut of the Russian Federation">Pilot-Cosmonaut of the Russian Federation</a> upon completion of their missions, often accompanied with the award of <a href="/wiki/Hero_of_the_Russian_Federation" title="Hero of the Russian Federation">Hero of the Russian Federation</a>.  This follows the practice established in the USSR where cosmonauts were usually awarded the title <a href="/wiki/Hero_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Hero of the Soviet Union">Hero of the Soviet Union</a>.\n</p><p>At NASA, those who complete astronaut candidate training receive a silver <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronaut_Badge#NASA_Astronaut_Pins" title="Astronaut Badge">lapel pin</a>. Once they have flown in space, they receive a gold pin. U.S. astronauts who also have active-duty military status receive a special qualification badge, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronaut_Badge" title="Astronaut Badge">Astronaut Badge</a>, after participation on a spaceflight. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> also presents an Astronaut Badge to its pilots who exceed 50 miles (80&#160;km) in altitude.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amf_space_mirror.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Amf_space_mirror.jpg/220px-Amf_space_mirror.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Amf_space_mirror.jpg/330px-Amf_space_mirror.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Amf_space_mirror.jpg/440px-Amf_space_mirror.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amf_space_mirror.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Space Mirror Memorial</div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Deaths">Deaths</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents#Astronaut_fatalities" title="List of spaceflight-related accidents and incidents">List of spaceflight-related accidents and incidents &#167;&#160;Astronaut fatalities</a></div>\n<p>Eighteen astronauts (fourteen men and four women) have lost their lives during four space flights. By nationality, thirteen were American (including one born in India), four were Russian (<a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>), and one was Israeli.\n</p><p>Eleven people (all men) have lost their lives training for spaceflight: eight Americans and three Russians. Six of these were in crashes of training jet aircraft, one drowned during water recovery training, and four were due to fires in pure oxygen environments.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Space_Mirror_Memorial" title="Space Mirror Memorial">Space Mirror Memorial</a>, which stands on the grounds of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_Space_Center" title="John F. Kennedy Space Center">John F. Kennedy Space Center</a> Visitor Complex, commemorates the lives of the men and women who have died during spaceflight and during training in the space programs of the United States. In addition to twenty NASA career astronauts, the memorial includes the names of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Air_Force" title="U.S. Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-15" title="X-15">X-15</a> test pilot, a U.S. Air Force officer who died while training for a then-classified military space program, and a civilian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spaceflight_participant" title="Spaceflight participant">spaceflight participant</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals"
